data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_potassium%20ion%20batteries">potassium ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Ultrafine+Red+Phosphorus&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_ultrafine%20red%20phosphorus">ultrafine red phosphorus</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Exclusively+Confinement&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_exclusively%20confinement">exclusively confinement</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=K4P3&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_K4P3">K<sub>4</sub>P<sub>3</sub></a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Stress+Simulation&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_stress%20simulation">stress simulation</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="NLM_p first">Potassium-ion batteries (PIBs) and sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have been considered as auspicious alternatives for state-of-the-art lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) where sustainable energy storage and commercial large scale use are concerned, primarily owing to their accessibility, similar redox potentials to Li, and low cost. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4" aria-label="reference 1 to 4">(1−4)</a> However, as compared with Li<sup>+</sup> (0.76 Å), the grander ionic radii of K<sup>+</sup> (1.38 Å) and Na<sup>+</sup> (1.02 Å) result in the different K<sup>+</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup> storage behaviors. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1,2)</a> To be specific, the commercial LIBs anode materials (<i>e.g</i>., graphite and silicon) fail in reversible intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> with traditional ester-based electrolyte. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref4" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3 ref4" aria-label="reference 3 and 4">(3,4)</a> In addition, PIBs and SIBs struggle with the unfavorable rate performance and limited cycle life as a result of large sizes of K<sup>+</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref6" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 5 and 6">(5,6)</a> Thus, it is urgent to construct a new anode material that is cheaper and safer and has a higher energy density with a longer cycling life.</div><div class="NLM_p">Red P-based composites have received great attention owing to their low cost, high specific capacity, and relatively safer operating potential. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref7" aria-label="reference 7">(7)</a> However, the fast capacity fading and poor high-rate performance, which are caused by severe electrode pulverization with subsequent unstable solid-electrolyte interphase (SEI) membranes during repeated charge–discharge processes, are still hindering further applications. The factors of pulverization and unstable SEI membranes are low electrical conductivity (∼10<sup>–14</sup> S cm<sup>–1</sup>) and large volume variation (∼400%) of P in SIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref8" aria-label="reference 8">(8)</a> More seriously, in addition to the above issues, other problems also exist in PIBs because the investigation of P is in infant stage, especially since the end potassiation product is still unclear, which is mainly a result of rich polymorphs of potassium phosphides (<i>e.g</i>.. K<sub>3</sub>P, KP, K<sub>4</sub>P<sub>3</sub>, K<sub>3</sub>P<sub>11</sub>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9" aria-label="reference 9">(9)</a> For instance, Wu, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref2" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref2" aria-label="reference 2">(2)</a> Chang, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref6" aria-label="reference 6">(6)</a> and Sultana <i>et al.</i> have reported that the theoretical specific capacity of P is 865 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (based on the KP), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10" aria-label="reference 10">(10)</a> while Yu’s group has demonstrated that the final potassiation product is K<sub>4</sub>P<sub>3</sub> with theoretical value of 1154 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref11" aria-label="reference 11">(11)</a> This deviation of capacity can be attributed to the reactivity of P with different size and morphology. Specifically speaking, the P with bulk type and nanoscale structure display different activity, which may lead to varied end products.</div><div class="NLM_p">To circumvent the above issues, many strategies, such as structural engineering, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref13 ref14" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref12 ref13 ref14" aria-label="reference 12 to 14">(12−14)</a> componential/interfacial modification, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18" aria-label="reference 15 to 18">(15−18)</a> and alloying strategy, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19 ref20" aria-label="reference 19 and 20">(19,20)</a> are employed. Among them, P is always manufactured to nanoscale and then loaded in carbonaceous materials to form nanostructured P–C composites. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref22 ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21 ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 21 to 23">(21−23)</a> For example, Yu’s group used the high-temperature infiltration method to confine P into an N-doped microporous carbon matrix to obtain P–C composites with excellent performance. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref8" aria-label="reference 8">(8)</a> Recently, Liu et al. synthesized a micromesoporous carbon nanosphere with large pore volume as a host for loading P to construct P-based composites, which exhibited a high capacity and rate performance. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1" aria-label="reference 1">(1)</a> On the basis of this progress, carbon scaffolds exert an effective role in enhancing electrical conductivity and alleviating volume change to some extent. It is safe to imagine that a much better electrochemical performance could be expected if red P is ultrafine and entirely restrained into nanoscale pores. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 23 and 24">(23,24)</a> Such an exclusively confined system eliminates the tremendous difference in size and morphology, giving an opportunity to precisely reveal K<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism. Besides, concerning the demand for future practical applications, a high loading content of P is required. Unfortunately, the high nano-P loading makes it agglomerate easily due to nanosized effects. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5" aria-label="reference 5">(5)</a> Therefore, increasing the loading amount and simultaneously ensuring the exclusive confinement of P into carbon scaffold raise more critical requirements for carbon scaffold as well as infiltration technology. In addition to regular requirements (such as the mechanical stability and high electrical conductivity) for carbon scaffolds, the hierarchical porous structure is highly demanded to meet comprehensive needs. In the hierarchical porous structure, macropores serve as mass/charge transfer media, while mesopores together with micropores are mainly regarded as the confinement and buffer the volume expansion effect. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> With respect to the loading process, high-temperature infiltration with subsequent removal of surface residual by CS<sub>2</sub>, instead of the ball-milling method, can ensure the good dispersion and exclusive confinement of ultrafine P. However, the existence of pores and P will result in low electronic conductivity and reactivity, hindering its high-rate performance. Therefore, it is important to simultaneously improve the reactivity and conductivity of P–C composites. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26 ref27" aria-label="reference 26 and 27">(26,27)</a> Taking the above-mentioned aspects into account, it is highly desirable to prepare a hierarchical porous carbon matrix with high reactivity and superior conductive as a host for exclusive confinement ultrafine P, which can obtain a high reversible capacity and initial Coulombic efficiency (CE), excellent rate, and long cycling P based anode for SIBs and PIBs.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Herein, a three-dimensional N-doped stripped-G CNT (denoted as N-SGCNT) with the hierarchical pores structure is designed to confine ultrafine-P. Besides having a high electrical conductivity by N-doping, this N-SGCNT matrix shows fascinating structural compatibility and stability due to the intrinsically coupled 3D frameworks, ensuring the high loading and exclusive restraint. With an initial optimal mass ratio, high-temperature infiltration plus subsequent facile removal are employed to confine ultrafine-P into a scaffold entirely. As expected, the P@N-SGCNT exhibits a high reversible specific capacity and long cycling stability in both SIBs and PIBs. Furthermore, benefitting from the exclusively confined P structure, the K-ion storage mechanism with an end product of K<sub>4</sub>P<sub>3</sub> is identified by experimental and theoretical results.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a shows the schematic diagram of synthesizing P@N-SGCNT through an oxidation–reduction (“O–R”) and vaporization–condensation (“V–C”) method. First, the pristine CNT undergoes a preoxidization process to obtain oxidized-CNT (O-CNT), introducing abundant defects and oxidative functional groups (OFG, <i>e.g</i>., −COO– and −OH) on the outer walls of each CNT. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> Next, an N-doping black sponge can be easily obtained during the hydrothermal process after adding pyrrole as N source to the above O–CNT solution. Upon this process, the OFG can be reduced, and simultaneously, the bonds of C–C, which was connected with OFG, are broken. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> Thus, the outer walls of CNT can be naturally stripped to mono 2D-graphene (2D-G) layers, enwrapping the inner 1D-CNT to construct a 3D intrinsically coupled structure. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> With subsequent freeze-drying, the N-SGCNT sponge with abundant edges, active sites, and cross-linked porous structure can be obtained. These active sites and porous structures are expected to absorb the red P vapor, which is confined into the empty spaces of the N-SGCNT host under a large driving force during the “V–C” process. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> To investigate the exclusive structure–activity relationship between P and N-SGCNT host on the capability of P@N-SGCNT, three composites (P<sub>1</sub>@N-SGCNT, P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT, and P<sub>3</sub>@N-SGCNT) are prepared by controlling the mass ratios of P and N-SGCNT (3:7, 5:5, and 7:3). We expect that the <i>in situ</i> cross-linked 1D@2D structure of the N-SGCNT matrix can prevent the agglomeration of P and ensure a larger amount of ultrafine P particles to be uniformly confined, thus enhancing the electrochemical performance of composites effectively.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/asset/images/medium/nn0c05951_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/asset/images/large/nn0c05951_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) Illustration of the synthetic route for P@N-SGCNT composites; SEM images of (b) P<sub>1</sub>@N-SGCNT, (c) P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT, and (d) P<sub>3</sub>@N-SGCNT; TEM images of (e) CNT, (f) O–CNT, and (g) P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT; (h) HAADF-STEM image of P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT and corresponding element mapping of (i) C, (j) P, (k) O, and (l) N.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/asset/images/large/nn0c05951_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.0c05951&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The morphologies of P@N-SGCNT composites are characterized by SEM and TEM. Three P@N-SGCNT samples all display 3D interconnected networks with abundant pores structure (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b–d), which are similar to the previous N-SGCNT scaffold (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/suppl_file/nn0c05951_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1b</a>). Furthermore, bulk P particles cannot be clearly observed in P<sub>1</sub>@N-SGCNT and P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT, which might be ascribed to the fact that the P is ultrafine and equably confined in the pores of these two composites (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b,c). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> Meanwhile, some bulk-type red P (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/suppl_file/nn0c05951_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1a</a>) is observed in P<sub>3</sub>@N-SGCNT, indicating the agglomeration of P (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>d). This phenomenon can also be obtained in the mechanical mixture sample (P<sub>2</sub>/N-SGCNT, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/suppl_file/nn0c05951_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1c</a>). Theus, suitable mass ratio and preparation processes both play a key role in maximizing the utilization of red P.</div><div class="NLM_p">TEM is further employed to acquire the microstructure information on products. In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>e, the outer and inner walls of pristine CNT are smooth. After oxidation, only the surface walls of O–CNT become rough. Therefore, the outer walls of O–CNT are easily tailored and stripped, while the inner walls keep the original tube shape (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>f). Compared with O–CNT, N-SGCNT (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/suppl_file/nn0c05951_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2a</a>) and three P@N-SGCNT (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>g, <b>S2b</b> and <b>S2c</b>) composites exhibit a similar 1D/2D interwoven structure, implying a stable covalent linkage between CNT and stripped-G under high temperature condensation treatment. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> In addition to structural stability, these 3D N-doped CNT bridged stripped-G building blocks are also expected to effectively facilitate ions/electron diffusion and promote the electrical conductivity. More importantly, bulk red P can only be observed in P<sub>3</sub>@N-SGCNT and can hardly be found in either P<sub>1</sub>@N-SGCNT or P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT, which is in agreement with the SEM results. Obtained from the EDX analysis, elemental mapping images of P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>i–l) show that carbon (C), P, oxygen (O), and N are uniformly dispersed without obvious phase aggregation.</div><div class="NLM_p">TGA is achieved in a nitrogen atmosphere to affirm the proportion of P<sub>red</sub> in each P@N-SGCNT composite. The actual red P contents in P<sub>1</sub>@N-SGCNT, P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT, and P<sub>3</sub>@N-SGCNT are 14.9%, 41.2%, and 59.7%, respectively, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/asset/images/medium/nn0c05951_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/asset/images/large/nn0c05951_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) TGA curves of P@N-SGCNT; (b) XRD patterns and (c) Raman spectra of the red P, N-SGCNT, and P@N-SGCNT; (d) pore size distribution of the N-SGCNT and P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT; (e) XPS survey spectra; high-resolution XPS spectrum of (f) P 2p and (g) N 1s of P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT; (h) FT-IR spectra of the P@N-SGCNT composites.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/asset/images/large/nn0c05951_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.0c05951&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b shows the XRD patterns of the commercial red P, N-SGCNT matrix, and corresponding three P@N-SGCNT composites. Three broad and weak peaks appear at 15°, 32°, and 55°, indicating a nature of red P. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> Besides, N-SGCNT shows characteristic peaks of (002) and (100) of carbon, appearing at 26° and 43°, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> Obviously, N-SGCNT, compared with pristine CNT (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/suppl_file/nn0c05951_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>), owns a low graphitization level without the palpable stacking structure. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> As for three P@N-SGCNT composites, the diffraction peaks of CNT and P can be observed, indicating that P is confined into the N-SGCNT host successfully.</div><div class="NLM_p">The structures of P, N-SGCNT, and three P@N-SGCNT composites are further analyzed by Raman spectra (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c). Several characteristic peaks of commercial P<sub>red</sub> appear from 500 to 300 cm<sup>–1</sup>, which is compatible with the previous literature. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> The fingerprint peaks for N-SGCNT are the graphitized G-band (∼1590 cm<sup>–1</sup>) and disordered D-band (∼1350 cm<sup>–1</sup>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> Interestingly, these representative peaks of P can only be observed in P<sub>3</sub>@N-SGCNT, while the signal is too weak to be found in P<sub>1</sub>@N-SGCNT and P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT, implying that the P is ultrafine and exclusively encapsulated within the nanopores of N-SGCNT during the high-temperature infiltration for P<sub>1</sub>@N-SGCNT and P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT. This result is dependable to the P@TBMC-2.4, as reported recently. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> Similar to P<sub>3</sub>@N-SGCNT, the typical peaks of P can be seen in P<sub>2</sub>/N-SGCNT mixture (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/suppl_file/nn0c05951_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>) because P can be hardly confined into the N-SGCNT matrix <i>via</i> a ball-milling process, as evidenced by SEM observation (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/suppl_file/nn0c05951_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2c</a>).</div><div class="NLM_p">Nitrogen adsorption and desorption measurements are utilized to quantify the pore volume, pore size distribution, and specific surface area (SSA) and of the N-SGCNT and P@N-SGCNT. In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d, a hierarchical structure with micromeso-macro pore size distribution is observed in N-SGCNT matrix. In addition, the SSA and pore volume of three P@N-SGCNT composites in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/suppl_file/nn0c05951_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a> are much smaller than the pristine N-SGCNT matrix. For instance, the pore volume is 1.13 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, and the SSA of N-SGCNT is 279.8 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>. The corresponding values of the P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT composite after loading of red P are dramatically decreased to 0.518 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> and 37.2 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Thus, it is believed that most of the inner pores of N-SGCNT are filled with ultrafine red P molecules. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref8" aria-label="reference 8">(8)</a> In addition, the mesopores can buffer the volume expansion of P, prohibiting severe capacity fade, while the macropores can facilitate the charge transportation owing to its large pore size and increase the active site of the material. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">More information on surface of the P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT is characterized by XPS analysis (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>e). XPS results show that the P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT composite mainly contains C, O, P, and N elements. Compared with red P (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/suppl_file/nn0c05951_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6a</a>), the P 2p high-resolution XPS profile of P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT is fitted by four peaks (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>f), which are dispensed to P–O (134.9 eV), P–C (134.2 eV), P–P(2p<sub>1/2</sub>) (130.8 eV), and P–P(2p<sub>3/2</sub>) (130.0 eV), respectively, demonstrating that the P is restrained by the P–O and P–C chemical interaction in N-SGCNT host. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10 ref22" aria-label="reference 10 and 22">(10,22)</a> The existence of small broad P–O bond in red P is ascribed to the oxidation of the surface partial P atoms. In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>g, three N peaks (quaternary-N at 401.0 eV, pyrrolic-N at 400.0 eV, and pyridinic-N at 398.6 eV) can be observed in P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38 ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38 ref39" aria-label="reference 38 and 39">(38,39)</a> in which pyridinic-N takes the relatively majority part acquired by further quantitative analysis. Higher N-doping content is energetically favorable for adsorbing abundant Na<sup>+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup>-ions and enhancing conductivity of carbon matrix (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/suppl_file/nn0c05951_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>), which is consistent with the result from Guo’s group. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> In addition, N-doping can introduce abundant defects and further increase the mass loading of P for P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT, which are compared with P@SGCNT (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/suppl_file/nn0c05951_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>). To further elucidate the chemical condition of P in the P@N-SGCNT samples, these composites are also detected by FT-IR spectra, and the result is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>h. A peak of ∼1261 cm<sup>–1</sup> is allotted to the C–N bonds. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> In addition, all samples feature four absorption peaks appearing at 1100, 1045, 947, and 801 cm<sup>–1</sup>, and they can be respectively recognized as the P–C and P–O bonds. This phenomenon is different from red P and P<sub>2</sub>/N-SGCNT mixture (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/suppl_file/nn0c05951_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6b</a>). Therefore, the P–O and P–C bonds in P@N-GCNT composites are generated during the “V–C” process. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> Combined with XPS analysis, we can deduce that the stable C–P, C–N, and P–O chemical bonds coexist in P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT, which can help stabilize the material microstructure.</div><div class="NLM_p">Thus, to obtain exclusively confined ultrafine P in the carbon scaffold, the preparation technology and rational initial ratio between P and scaffold both play the key role. In other words, simply physical milling as well as excess the red P lead to obvious particles aggregation and further accumulate outside the scaffolds. Optimal ratio and precise treatment can confine P to the nanoscale and simultaneously avoid P aggregation on the surface as well as remove the residual chemically unstable and highly toxic white P. Moreover, the red P is chemically anchored on N-SGCNT by solid P–O and P–C bonds. Therefore, the superior electrochemical performance is rather expected in exclusive confinement P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT with a maximum loading amount of red P.</div><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical performance of P@N-SGCNT when applied as SIBs anodes is estimated in 2032 coin-type half-cells. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a presents the CV curves of the initial five cycles when using the P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT anode. A broad peak at 1.0–1.5 V could be observed only in the first cathodic scan, which is associated with the Na<sup>+</sup> liquid electrolyte decomposition and the stable SEI film formation on the electrode surface. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> In comparison with N-SGCNT (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/suppl_file/nn0c05951_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>), two additional broad peaks located at ∼0.34 V and ∼0.05 V are observed in the P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT during the subsequent cathodic reduction process, which are ascribed to the successive formation of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>P (<i>x</i> = 1–3) intermediates and the process of intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> into the N-SGCNT, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> In addition, there is a difference between the CV curves of P<sub>1</sub>@N-SGCNT/P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT and P<sub>3</sub>@N-SGCNT in the second discharge process (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/suppl_file/nn0c05951_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10a</a>), which may due to the reaction kinetics of the formation of intermediate products (Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>P). The kinetic analysis will be performed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>. As to anodic scans, three peaks at ∼0.59, ∼0.65, and ∼0.86 V could be observed in the first and following cycles, as a result of the gradually Na<sup>+</sup> extraction from total charged Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>P phase to intermediate products (Na<sub><i>y</i></sub>P, 1 ≤ y ≤ <i>x</i> ≤ 3) and finally to P<sub>red</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> In <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/suppl_file/nn0c05951_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>, the P<sub>1</sub>@N-SGCNT and P<sub>3</sub>@N-SGCNT anodes exhibit similar CV curves, except for the largest area reduction of P<sub>3</sub>@N-SGCNT, implying that bulk P particles on surface result in a huge irreversible capacity loss in P<sub>3</sub>@N-SGCNT. In contrast, benefited from the uniformly distributed ultrafine P and exclusively confinement into defects/pores of N-SGCNT matrix, P<sub>1</sub>@N-SGCNT and P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT both exhibit a high reversibility of the multistep reaction from the second cycle onward.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/asset/images/medium/nn0c05951_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/asset/images/large/nn0c05951_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Electrochemical performance in SIBs: (a) CV curves of P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT within the voltage window of 0.01–2.0 V at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>; (b) GDC profiles of P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>; (c) comparison of the initial CE of P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT and previous reported P/C materials; (d) rate capabilities and (e) cycling properties of N-SGCNT and three P@N-SGCNT anodes; (f) cycling property of P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT at 1000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/asset/images/large/nn0c05951_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.0c05951&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/asset/images/medium/nn0c05951_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/asset/images/large/nn0c05951_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) Nyquist plots of three P@N-SGCNT anodes at the voltage of 0.01 V. (b) Liner relation of ω<sup>1/2</sup><i>vs</i> −Z′′; (c) GITT profiles of three P@N-SGCNT anodes; Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient as a function of the state of (d) sodiation and (e) desodiation process.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/asset/images/large/nn0c05951_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.0c05951&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b exhibits the galvanostatic discharge/charge (GDC) curves of P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT during the initial three cycles under 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. The plateaus trait in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b is unswerving with the above CV results. The specific capacity of P@N-SGCNT is calculated based on P <i>via</i> the equation of “<i>C</i><sub>P</sub> = [<i>C</i><sub>total</sub> – <i>C</i><sub>N-SGCNT</sub> × <i>W</i><sub>N-SGCNT</sub>]/<i>W</i><sub>P</sub>”, where <i>W</i> and <i>C</i> are weight percent (based on the TGA) and specific capacity, respectively. Ultrahigh discharge capacities of 3233, 2576, and 2490 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> for P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT are obtained through the first three cycles, respectively. The gradually stabilized capacities from the second cycle onward are near to the theoretical specific capacity (2596 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>). Moreover, the initial CE of P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT reaches 80.0% and then rises to more than 98.6% at the following cycle, which is much higher than that of the related P/C works (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c, detailed information shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/suppl_file/nn0c05951_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref3 ref5 ref7 ref8 ref21 ref23 ref26 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref42 ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref3 ref5 ref7 ref8 ref21 ref23 ref26 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref42 ref43" aria-label="reference 1, 3, 5, 7, 8, 21, 23, 26, 33 to 35, 42 and 43">(1,3,5,7,8,21,23,26,33−35,42,43)</a> Similarly, the P<sub>1</sub>@N-SGCNT containing 14.9 wt % ultrafine red P exhibits high capacities (3201, 2519, and 2470 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) upon the first three cycles with an initial CE of 78.4% (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/suppl_file/nn0c05951_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11c</a>). In sharp contrast, the initial discharge and charge capacities are 3136 and 1412 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> for P<sub>3</sub>@N-SGCNT, respectively, corresponding to a very low initial CE of 45.0% (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/suppl_file/nn0c05951_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11d</a>), which may be ascribed to the excess bulk P. This phenomenon can also be found in the N-SGCNT matrix (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/suppl_file/nn0c05951_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11a</a>) and P<sub>2</sub>/N-SGCNT mixture (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/suppl_file/nn0c05951_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11b</a>).</div><div class="NLM_p">The rate capabilities of P@N-SGCNT electrodes at an incremental density from 0.1 to 2.0 A g<sup>–1</sup> are compared in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d. As a result of the outstanding electrical conductivity of N-SGCNT matrix and the high loading quantity of ultrafine P, brilliant specific capacities of 2480, 2239, 2072, 1931, and 1770 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> for the P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT can be reached at 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, and 2.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Furthermore, when the testing current density returns to the original condition of 100 A g<sup>–1</sup>, a high discharge capacity of 2366 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 40 cycles can be sustained, exhibiting a high quality for an abuse staying power with different testing condition. In the interim, rate-cycling capabilities of P<sub>1</sub>@N-SGCNT are superior than P<sub>3</sub>@N-SGCNT, but a slightly lower than P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT. The rational explanation to this phenomenon is that the lower loading amount of red P causes loose contact with the scaffold substrate upon subsequent cycling.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e exhibits the long cycle stability behaviors of the P@N-SGCNT composites. It clearly shows that P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT demonstrates the most exceptional cycling stability. Gone through the discharge capacity drop to ∼2130 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after the initial 100 cycles, the capacity returns back to more than 2322 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 300 cycles and sustains at 2310 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with further cycling to 500 cycles. This reversible capacity is higher than other previous works (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/suppl_file/nn0c05951_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>). This phenomenon of dropping first and then rising is also mentioned in other literature. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> Additionally, the CE are all close to 100%. As a comparison, P<sub>1</sub>@N-SGCNT and P<sub>3</sub>@N-SGCNT only display capacities of 2001 and 1132 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> under the same current density after 500 cycles. More importantly, P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT also exhibits the highest capacity based on the weight of the whole material than P<sub>1</sub>@N-SGCNT and P<sub>3</sub>@N-SGCNT (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/suppl_file/nn0c05951_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12</a>), which is ascribed to the exclusively confined P structure in the P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT. In addition, P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT shows excellent cyclability at 1000 mA g<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>f). The specific capacity can maintain at 1936 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with CE of 100% even cycling up to 2000 cycles. Judging by the P–C composite anodes covered in the literature, the cycling performance of P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT shows the best results to date. On the basis of the above performance comparisons, the following aspects can be deduced: (i) With respect to initial CE, P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT shows the highest value, in line with application demands. For the first cycle, three samples with different loading amounts exhibit similar discharge capacity, while the biggest difference lies in the charge capacity. Excess loading (P<sub>3</sub>@N-SGCNT) leads to a large portion of external surface bulk P, which may lose electrochemical activity, and less participation in the following reactions. In contrast, this deterioration could be alleviated by decreasing the loading amount. However, this loading decrease will cause a new problem in that the structure is too relaxed to confine red P, which means it is hardly ensuring the optimal contact between P and carbon frameworks. (ii) There exists an activation period in exclusively confined P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT, which is absent in P<sub>1</sub>@N-SGCNT and P<sub>3</sub>@N-SGCNT. It is implied that at the initial stage P and N-SGCNT are in close contact without extra space therein, and upon subsequent cycling, accompanied by the volume change, the activity of red P is further stimulated. Thus, N-SGCNT is strong enough to endure such a volume change.</div><div class="NLM_p">Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) is directed to analyze the charge-transfer kinetics and Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion ability of electrodes. Obtained from <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/suppl_file/nn0c05951_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S3</a>, the charge-transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) of P<sub>1</sub>@N-SGCNT and P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT are both lower than the P<sub>3</sub>@N-SGCNT by using the equivalent circuit in the inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a, which indicates that (i) nano-P particles can effectually reduce the charge transfer impedance of the whole materials and (ii) increasing the mass loading of nano-P in the same matrix has little effect on the impedance. In addition, the diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i>) of Na<sup>+</sup> can be investigated by using the below three equations: <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="ueq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/asset/images/medium/nn0c05951_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i3"></div><div class="label"></div></div></span>The parameters are shown in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/suppl_file/nn0c05951_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>. After calculations, the σ (Warburg coefficient) values of P<sub>1</sub>@N-SGCNT, P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT, and P<sub>3</sub>@N-SGCNT electrodes in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b are 63, 65, and 229 Ω S<sup>–1/2</sup>, respectively. Obviously, P<sub>1</sub>@N-SGCNT and P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT display a similar σ, demonstrating that increasing the quality of nano-P does not affect the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion rate of composites. Furthermore, the σ-values of P<sub>1</sub>@N-SGCNT and P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT are smaller than P<sub>3</sub>@N-SGCNT, revealing that both of them possess a faster Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion rate.</div><div class="NLM_p">A galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) can be employed to deeply reveal the kinetics about the Na<sup>+</sup> migration coefficient at the state of sodiation/desodiation. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c shows the potential response of the three P@N-SGCNT electrodes during the sodiation/desodiation process. The potential change during each relaxation period represents the overpotential at the corresponding sodiation/desodiation stage. Plainly, the diffusion in all three P@N-SGCNT composites shows the same trend, suggesting a similar Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism for P<sub>1</sub>@N-SGCNT, P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT, and P<sub>3</sub>@N-SGCNT. After calculations, the values of <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> of P<sub>1</sub>@N-SGCNT, P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT, and P<sub>3</sub>@N-SGCNT electrodes are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d,e. Compared to P<sub>3</sub>@N-SGCNT, both P<sub>1</sub>@N-SGCNT and P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT show a higher <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> during the sodiation and desodiation processes, suggesting faster kinetics for P<sub>1</sub>@N-SGCNT and P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT, which can be ascribed to the 1D@2D conductive network and nano-P with stable C–N/P–C/P–O–C bonds. This result is accordance with EIS analysis. In addition, D<sub>Na+</sub> displays an obvious change at ∼0.4 and ∼0.17 V in the sodiation process (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d) and ∼0.5 and ∼0.7 V in the desodiation process (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>e), exhibiting slow kinetics performance. Owing to the reaction between Na<sup>+</sup> and P@N-SGCNT occurring in these potentials, the minimal <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> corresponds to the phase transformation kinetics. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45 ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45 ref46" aria-label="reference 45 and 46">(45,46)</a> Furthermore, the sweep voltammetry is employed to investigate the kinetics performance. After calculations, P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT exhibits the fastest Na<sup>+</sup> migration (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/suppl_file/nn0c05951_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13</a>), which benefits from the exclusively confined nano-P structure.</div><div class="NLM_p">Mass loading of the electrode in basic frontier research is a key technical parameter for industrial production, but it remains unnoticed. Thus, the effect of different mass loading of P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT varied from 1.3 to 4.3 mg cm<sup>–2</sup> on the corresponding Na<sup>+</sup> storage performance is investigated. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a shows the rate performance of P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT with different mass loading. From that, the P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT anode displays much less capacity degradation induced by mass loading at low current densities, and a specific capacity of 2218 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (1793 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) is maintained with 4.3 mg cm<sup>–2</sup> loading at 0.1 mA g<sup>–1</sup> (0.2 mA g<sup>–1</sup>). This value is only 10.5% (19.8%) less than the 1.3 mg cm<sup>–2</sup> mass loading for the same electrode architecture. Even under 2.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT electrode with 4.3 mg cm<sup>–2</sup> loading retains a capacity of ∼500 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. What’s more, the capacity retention of P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT with 1.3/2.1/2.9 mg cm<sup>–2</sup> loading at the various current densities is higher than 50% (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b), demonstrating the robust 3D pore structure and uniform distribution of nano-P particles in the N-SGCNT host. The P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT electrode also exhibits excellent performance when suffering a high current density of 1.0 A g<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c). The great specific capacities of 1904, 1466, 1317, and 837 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 300 cycles can be obtained with 1.3, 2.1, 2.9, and 4.3 mg cm<sup>–2</sup> loading, respectively.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/asset/images/medium/nn0c05951_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/asset/images/large/nn0c05951_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) Rate capabilities of P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT anodes with different mass loading in Na-ion half-cell; (b) capacity retention at various current densities; (c) cycling property of P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT with different mass loading at 1.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>; (d) schematic illustration of assembled Na(Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>)O<sub>2</sub>||P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT Na-ion full cell and a “starlike” LED bulb lighted by this full cell; (e) rate performance; (f) GDC profiles at various current densities at the range of 1.5–4.0 V; (g) cycling performance of Na-ion full cell.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/asset/images/large/nn0c05951_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.0c05951&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Furthermore, the Na-ion full cell is assembled by using Na(Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>)O<sub>2</sub> as the cathode and P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT as the anode, and the schematic illustration is displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d. The Na(Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>)O<sub>2</sub>||P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT full battery can support a “starlike” LED bulb, corroborating its promising practical potential. Before assembly, the Na(Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>)O<sub>2</sub> and P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT electrodes are precycled in a half cell, respectively, which can eliminate the irreversible capacity in the first several cycles and replenish abundant Na-ions. The GDC profile of Na(Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>)O<sub>2</sub> is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/suppl_file/nn0c05951_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S14</a>. In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>e, it reveals initial charge and discharge capacities of 2487.5 and 1966 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, which leads to a CE of ∼79% based on the calculation of P. In addition, reversible capacities of 2002, 1690, 1389, 1186, and 957 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> can be acquired at 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, and 2.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Notably, an excellent capacity of 1601 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> is returned after 60 cycles when the specific current density back to 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, indicating a high rate capability and promising reversibility of the full cell. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>f shows the GDC profiles of the Na(Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>)O<sub>2</sub>||P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT full cell under different rates from 0.1 to 2.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>, and the main discharge/charge platforms and shapes have not changed significantly. As expected, the Na-ion full cell can perform an excellent cycle performance at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>g). It can still deliver a high capacity of 1576 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 140 cycles. Apparently, P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT shows a prodigious potential in practical applications based on its high mass loading and full-cell performance.</div><div class="NLM_p">Inspired by above decent SIB performance of P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT composite, we are challenged to exploit its potential in the direction of K<sup>+</sup> storage, in which the atomic radius is larger than Na<sup>+</sup>. To the best of our knowledge, only several examples of P–C composites have been reported for PIBs, and most of the composites were prepared by ball-milling techniques. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref10 ref47" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5 ref10 ref47" aria-label="reference 5, 10 and 47">(5,10,47)</a> As mentioned before, such a physical mixture is unable to prevent the agglomeration of P, resulting in lower CE and specific capacity. More importantly, compared with the Li<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism of P–C anode materials, the K<sup>+</sup>-storage mechanism is still unclear. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9" aria-label="reference 9">(9)</a> To deeply comprehend the potassiation evolution of P, density functional theory (DFT) calculations are employed (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a) first. On the basis of the binary phase diagram of K–P, four phases (K<sub>2</sub>P<sub>3</sub>, KP, K<sub>4</sub>P<sub>3</sub>, and K<sub>3</sub>P) are implemented in this calculation. The lowest formation energy of −0.88 eV (per P atom) is observed for the phase of K<sub>4</sub>P<sub>3</sub>, which is larger than K<sub>2</sub>P<sub>3</sub> (−0.73 eV), KP (−0.83 eV), and K<sub>3</sub>P (−0.84 eV), signifying that it is the most thermodynamically stable structure and the end product upon potassiation process. Furthermore, the electrochemical performance of P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT as PIBs anode is also assessed in K-ion half-cells. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b displays the GDC profiles of P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT at 0.05 A g<sup>–1</sup>, and the first discharge capacity of 1080 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> can be obtained, which is near to the theoretical specific capacity of K<sub>4</sub>P<sub>3</sub> (1154 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>). This phenomenon can also be obtained from <i>ex situ</i> XRD analysis (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/suppl_file/nn0c05951_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S15</a>). Thus, we believe that the reaction of “3P + 4K<sup>+</sup> + 4e<sup>–</sup> ⇆ K<sub>4</sub>P<sub>3</sub>” is a rational potassiation process, which is suitable for the recent results from Koratkar’s group. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> Furthermore, P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT with an exclusively confined nano-P structure exhibits a high initial CE of 67.4%, which is much higher than that of the previous P/C works in PIBs (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/suppl_file/nn0c05951_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S4</a>). The capacity attenuation is due to the SEI film formation. In the second cycle, the CE becomes 92.7% when the discharge/charge capacities are 762/701 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. In comparison, the N-SGCNT anodes display bad capacities in the initial three cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/suppl_file/nn0c05951_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S16</a>). The nearly overlapped anodic CV curves also show a good cycling stability of the P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT anodes in PIBs (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/suppl_file/nn0c05951_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S17</a>). Importantly, two broad peaks located at ∼0.51 and ∼0.23 V are observed during the cathodic reduction process, which can be ascribed to the continuous formation from P to K<sub>4</sub>P<sub>3</sub>. For the anodic scans, two peaks arise at ∼0.81 V and ∼1.19 V and are linked with the depotassiation process.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/asset/images/medium/nn0c05951_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/asset/images/large/nn0c05951_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. Electrochemical performance in PIBs: (a) formation energies of different alloys calculated by DFT; (b) GDC profiles of P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT at 0.05 A g<sup>–1</sup>; (c) rate capabilities of CNT, N-SGCNT, and P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT; (d) cycling properties of the P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT; (e) GITT profiles of the P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT anode. The K<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient as a function of the state of the (f) potassiation and (g) depotassiation process.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/asset/images/large/nn0c05951_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.0c05951&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The rate capability of P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT in PIBs with the current densities ranging from 0.05 to 2.0 A g<sup>–1</sup> is also examined. The capacities of 707, 554, 476, 431, 387, and 354 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> are attained at 0.05, 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, and 2.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively (see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>c). The specific capacity of 575 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> when the current density returns to 0.05 A g<sup>–1</sup> can be observed for P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT after 60 cycles, revealing an excellent advantage for an abuse endurance under different current densities in PIBs. CNT, N-SGCNT, P@CNT, and P@SGCNT show very low rate performance compared with P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/suppl_file/nn0c05951_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S18</a>). The P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT also displays brilliant cyclability with high capacity in PIBs. In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>d, the reversible capacity still maintains 514 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup> with the CE of 99.4%, even with cycling up to 200 cycles. In addition, P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT also shows excellent cyclability at 2.0 A g<sup>–1</sup> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/suppl_file/nn0c05951_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S19</a>). The capacity after cycling up to 1000 cycles could stably sustain at 319 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with CE close to 100%. The present composite denotes one of the best properties among all the related carbon-based electrode materials on the basis of cycling stability and capacity for PIBs (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/suppl_file/nn0c05951_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S5</a>).</div><div class="NLM_p">GITT can also be used to reveal the kinetics about K<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient through the potassiation/depotassiation process. In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>e, the potential change of P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT within each relaxation circle expresses the overpotential at the homologous potassiation/depotassiation stage. After calculations, the values of <i>D</i><sub>K+</sub> of P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>f,g. Although the radius of K<sup>+</sup> is bigger than Na<sup>+</sup>, <i>D</i><sub>K+</sub> lies in the range of 10<sup>–11</sup>–10<sup>–13</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>, which is similar to that of <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub>. This phenomenon can also be obtained from the EIS analysis (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/suppl_file/nn0c05951_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S20</a>). Therefore, the P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT anode still exhibits fast kinetics property, which can be attributed to the 1D@2D conductive network and nano-P particles confined in the C matrix uniformly as well as the stable C–N/P–C/P–O–C bonds.</div><div class="NLM_p">The fast Na<sup>+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup> diffusion and high conductivity of the P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT composite is not only related to the nano-P particles but also to the 3D <i>in situ</i> cross-linked carbon matrix. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49 ref50" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref49 ref50" aria-label="reference 49 and 50">(49,50)</a> To reveal the superiority of the “exclusively confined nano-P system”, the stress distribution based on a finite element method is conducted. Obviously, particle size and mass loading of P play an important role in this work. Therefore, three models are employed (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>a). P<sub>1</sub>@C (≈P<sub>1</sub>@N-SGCNT) contains one nano-P particle and shows a lot of remaining space, P<sub>2</sub>@C (≈P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT) displays exclusively confined nano-P structure, and P<sub>3</sub>@C (≈P<sub>3</sub>@N-SGCNT) contains bulk-P. After sodiation/potassiation, the stress exhibits more uniform presence in P<sub>1</sub>@C and P<sub>2</sub>@C, while it highly concentrates at the surface in P<sub>3</sub>@C, indicating that the nano-P structure can effectively alleviate huge stress caused by volume change, thereby promoting long-cycling and rate performance. Therefore, loading nano-P as much as possible is beneficial to improve performance of P@N-SGCNT. Throughout results above, it is palpable that P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT displays the best properties among these three P@N-SGCNT materials. The ameliorative electrochemical performance for P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT is clarified here with the assistance of schematic drawings given in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>b. For P<sub>1</sub>@N-SGCNT, the loading amount of P is 14.9%, and it is ultrafine and homogeneously nanoconfined in N-SGCNT matrix. Such nano-P results in an amply enhanced property, especially the initial CE. However, the low amount of nano-P becomes an insufficiency with the result of scarcely using the preponderance of P that contains a high theoretical capacity. As for P<sub>3</sub>@N-SGCNT, the P loading is as high as 59.7%, but agglomeration converts the problem. At the same time, the electrochemical activity is poor and easy to pulverize, leading to electrical contact loss during cycling, which decays fast. In comparison, P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT possesses high loading of ultrafine red P (41.2%), which is primarily accountable for the excellent electrochemical properties. Specifically, the 3D cross-linked host delivers sufficient available space to accompany the volume inflation of P during dis-/charge process and owns high electrical conductivity. The existence of P–O/P–C/N–C chemical bonds also improves the electrical conductivity of the overall material and increases the endurance for its pulverization. The above synergetic effect verifies the awesome Na<sup>+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup> storage property with high initial CE, excellent reversible specific capacity, and outstanding cycling capability of P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/asset/images/medium/nn0c05951_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/asset/images/large/nn0c05951_0007.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. (a) Stress distribution in three P@C composites before and after sodiation/potassiation. (b) Schematic illustration of the reversible reaction mechanism of the P@N-SGCNT composites.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/asset/images/large/nn0c05951_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.0c05951&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i4">Conclusion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, the precise selection of N-doped stripped-G CNT (N-SGCNT) as a carbon framework, combined with additional surface cleaning, is employed to exclusively confine the nano-P with a loading amount as high as 41.2 wt % to construct P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT composites. To implement entire confinements, this N-SGCNT framework not only meets the traditional requirements such as high electrical conductivity and hierarchical pores to ensure high P loading but also provides structural compatibility and stability upon cycling, due to the intrinsically coupled 3D frameworks. As expected, the P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT exhibits high reversible capacities of 2480 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> for SIBs and 762 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> for PIBs, rate capabilities of 1770 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> for SIBs and 354 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> for PIBs at 2.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>, and a long cycling stability (a capacity of 1936 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 2000 cycles for SIBs and 319 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 1000 cycles for PIBs). Additionally, due to the exclusively confined P structure, K-ion storage mechanism with the end product of K<sub>4</sub>P<sub>3</sub> is identified by experimental and theoretical results.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i5">Methods</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec99" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> Synthesis of the P@N-SGCNT Composites</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The P@N-SGCNT are prepared by infiltration of the P molecule into N-SGCNT (mass ratios of N-SGCNT and P is 1:1, 7:3, and 3:7) at a quartz capsule under 700 °C for 6 h. After that, P@N-SGCNT can be obtained after cooling to 25 °C. P@N-SGCNT needs to be washed with the CS<sub>2</sub> solution one time quickly to remove white P, which is created at the condensation process. Detailed information about the preparation of carbon host and composites, physical characterizations, and electrochemical measurements is shown in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/suppl_file/nn0c05951_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>.</div></div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Detailed information about the preparation process; GITT and EIS measurements; finite element method analysis; SEM and TEM images; XRD and Raman spectra; N<sub>2</sub> adsorption isotherms and pore size distribution; XPS and FT-IR of red P; DFT calculations; TGA of P@SGCNT; CV curves; GDC profiles; tables of comparison of the initial CE and electrochemical performance; cycling property of P<sub>2</sub>@N-SGCNT at 2000 mA g<sup>–1</sup> in PIBs; EIS analysis; sweep CV curves; <i>ex situ</i> XRD (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/suppl_file/nn0c05951_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.0c05951/suppl_file/nn0c05951_si_001.pdf">nn0c05951_si_001.pdf (2.03 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsnano.0c05951" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Encapsulating Red Phosphorus in Ultralarge Pore Volume Hierarchical Porous Carbon Nanospheres for Lithium/Sodium-Ion Half/Full Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Bingqiu; Zhang, Qi; Li, Lu; Jin, Zhanshuang; Wang, Chungang; Zhang, Lingyu; Su, Zhong-Min</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">13</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">11</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">13513-13523</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ANCAC3</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1936-0851</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Red phosphorus (P) has been recognized as a promising material for lithium/sodium-ion batteries (LIBs/SIBs) because of their high theor. capacity.  However, tremendous vol. variation and low cond. limit its widespread applications.  Hence, we design and synthesize uniformly distributed honeycomb-like hierarchical micro-mesoporous carbon nanospheres (HHPCNSs) with ultralarge pore vol. (3.258 cm3 g-1) on a large scale through a facile way.  The large pore vol. provides enough space for loading of P and the expansion of P, and the uniform distribution of the micro-mesopores enables the red P to load uniformly.  The resulting HHPCNSs/P composite exhibits extremely high capacity (2463.8 and 2367.6 mA h g-1 at 0.1 A g-1 for LIBs and SIBs, resp.), splendid rate performance (842.2 and 831.1 mA h g-1 at 10 A g-1 for LIBs and SIBs, resp.) and superior cycling stability (1201.6 and 938.4 mA h g-1 at 2 and 5 A g-1 after 1000 cycles for LIBs and 1269.4 and 861.8 mA h g-1 at 2 and 5 A g-1 after 1000 cycles for SIBs, resp.).  More importantly, when coupled with LiFePO4 and Na3V2(PO4)3 cathode, lithium/sodium-ion full batteries display high capacity and superior rate and cycling performances, revealing the practicability of the HHPCNSs/P composite.  The exceptional electrochem. performance is caused by the honeycomb-like carbon network with ultralarge pore vol., uniformly distributed hierarchical micro-mesoporous nanostructure, outstanding electronic cond., and excellent nanostructural stability, which is much better than currently reported P/C materials for both LIBs and SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref2" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201900343"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xing, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lam, C.                         cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Advanced anode for sodium-ion battery with promising long cycling stability achieved by tuning phosphorus-carbon nanostructures</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yu, Zhaoxin; Song, Jiangxuan; Wang, Daiwei; Wang, Donghai</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">40</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">550-558</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NEANCA</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2211-2855</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Phosphorus is considered as a promising anode for Na-ion battery because of its high theor. capacity of 2595 mAh g-1.  In this study, two phosphorus-carbon (P-C) composites with well-controlled compns. and nanostructures of P and C have been developed: P@YP composite with P confined within porous structure of YP-80F carbon and P@CNT with unconfined P deposited on the surface of carbon nanotube.  The structure and electrochem. performance of these two composites have been studied to illustrate the effect of nanostructures of both C and P.  P@YP composite with appropriate amt. of P confined in nanopores can accommodate its large vol. change upon sodiation/desodiation and enable a stable solid-electrolyte interphase (SEI), ensuring an excellent long-term cycling stability with superior capacity retention of 92% after 100 cycles and 46% after 1000 cycles.  In contrast, the P@CNT composite with unconfined P nanostructures shows a rapid capacity decay with capacity retention of ∼ 40.6% after 100 cycles, most likely due to unstable SEI during cycling, caused by the large vol. changes of unconfined P in the P@CNT composite.  The well-designed nanostructured P-C composite with P confined within porous structure of carbon is demonstrated to greatly enhance the electrochem. performance, leading to promising long-term cycling stability.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref4" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201701648"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Short-Range Order in Mesoporous Carbon Boosts Potassium-Ion Battery Performance</span>. <i>Adv. Energy Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>8</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1701648</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/aenm.201701648</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D8%26publication_year%3D2018%26pages%3D1701648%26journal%3DAdv.%2BEnergy%2BMater.%26author%3DW.%2BWang%26author%3DJ.%2BZhou%26author%3DZ.%2BWang%26author%3DL.%2BZhao%26author%3DP.%2BLi%26author%3DY.%2BYang%26author%3DC.%2BYang%26author%3DH.%2BHuang%26author%3DS.%2BGuo%26title%3DShort-Range%2BOrder%2Bin%2BMesoporous%2BCarbon%2BBoosts%2BPotassium-Ion%2BBattery%2BPerformance%26doi%3D10.1002%252Faenm.201701648&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsnano.0c05951&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Faenm.201701648&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord empty" id="
